      <p>
       <b>
        Linux
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="nowrap">
         (
         <span class="unicode haudio">
          <span class="fn">
           <span style="white-space:nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/File:Linus-linux.ogg" title="About this sound">
             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
            </a>
           </span>
           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/03/Linus-linux.ogg" title="Linus-linux.ogg">
            listen
           </a>
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          LIN
         </span>
         -əks
        </i>
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pronunciation-2_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pronunciation-2-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foldoc09Jun06_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Foldoc09Jun06-12">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is a family of
       <a href="/wiki/Free_and_open-source_software" title="Free and open-source software">
        free and open-source software
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">
        operating systems
       </a>
       built around the
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">
        Linux kernel
       </a>
       . Typically, Linux is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Package_management_system" title="Package management system">
        packaged
       </a>
       in a form known as a
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_distribution" title="Linux distribution">
        Linux distribution
       </a>
       (or
       <i>
        distro
       </i>
       for short) for both desktop and server use. The defining component of a Linux distribution is the
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">
        Linux kernel
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernel_(computing)" title="Kernel (computing)">
        operating system kernel
       </a>
       first released on September 17, 1991, by
       <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">
        Linus Torvalds
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Linux was originally developed for
       <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">
        personal computers
       </a>
       based on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intel_x86" title="Intel x86">
        Intel x86
       </a>
       architecture, but has since been
       <a href="/wiki/Porting" title="Porting">
        ported
       </a>
       to more
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_hardware_platforms" title="Computer hardware platforms">
        platforms
       </a>
       than any other operating system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because of the dominance of the Linux kernel-based
       <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">
        Android
       </a>
       OS on
       <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">
        smartphones
       </a>
       , Linux has the
       <a href="/wiki/Usage_share_of_operating_systems" title="Usage share of operating systems">
        largest
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Installed_base" title="Installed base">
        installed base
       </a>
       of all
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General-purpose_operating_system" title="General-purpose operating system">
        general-purpose operating systems
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marketshare.hitslink.com_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-marketshare.hitslink.com-18">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Linux is also the leading operating system on
       <a href="/wiki/Server_(computing)" title="Server (computing)">
        servers
       </a>
       and other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_iron" title="Big iron">
        big iron
       </a>
       systems such as
       <a href="/wiki/Mainframe_computer" title="Mainframe computer">
        mainframe computers
       </a>
       , and the only OS used on
       <a href="/wiki/TOP500" title="TOP500">
        TOP500
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer" title="Supercomputer">
        supercomputers
       </a>
       (since November 2017, having before gradually eliminated all competitors).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rules_supercomputers_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rules_supercomputers-20">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is used by around 2.3% of
       <a href="/wiki/Desktop_computer" title="Desktop computer">
        desktop computers
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Netmarketshare.com_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Netmarketshare.com-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Chromebook" title="Chromebook">
        Chromebook
       </a>
       , which runs the Linux kernel-based
       <a href="/wiki/Chrome_OS" title="Chrome OS">
        Chrome OS
       </a>
       , dominates the US
       <a href="/wiki/K%E2%80%9312" title="K–12">
        K–12
       </a>
       education market and represents nearly 20% of the sub-$300
       <a href="/wiki/Laptop" title="Laptop">
        notebook
       </a>
       sales in the US.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Linux also runs on
       <a href="/wiki/Embedded_system" title="Embedded system">
        embedded systems
       </a>
       —devices whose
       <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">
        operating system
       </a>
       is typically built into the
       <a href="/wiki/Firmware" title="Firmware">
        firmware
       </a>
       and is highly tailored to the system. This includes
       <a href="/wiki/TiVo" title="TiVo">
        TiVo
       </a>
       and similar
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_video_recorder" title="Digital video recorder">
        DVR
       </a>
       devices, network
       <a href="/wiki/Router_(computing)" title="Router (computing)">
        routers
       </a>
       , facility automation controls, televisions,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">
        video game consoles
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Smartwatch" title="Smartwatch">
        smartwatches
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LinuxDevices_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LinuxDevices-26">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many smartphones and
       <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">
        tablet computers
       </a>
       run Android and other Linux derivatives.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The development of Linux is one of the most prominent examples of free and open-source
       <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">
        software
       </a>
       collaboration. The underlying
       <a href="/wiki/Source_code" title="Source code">
        source code
       </a>
       may be used, modified and distributed—commercially or non-commercially—by anyone under the terms of its respective licenses, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_General_Public_License" title="GNU General Public License">
        GNU General Public License
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Some of the most popular and mainstream Linux distributions
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       are
       <a href="/wiki/Arch_Linux" title="Arch Linux">
        Arch Linux
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/CentOS" title="CentOS">
        CentOS
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Debian" title="Debian">
        Debian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fedora_(operating_system)" title="Fedora (operating system)">
        Fedora
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Gentoo_Linux" title="Gentoo Linux">
        Gentoo Linux
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_Mint" title="Linux Mint">
        Linux Mint
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mageia" title="Mageia">
        Mageia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/OpenSUSE" title="OpenSUSE">
        openSUSE
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_(operating_system)" title="Ubuntu (operating system)">
        Ubuntu
       </a>
       , together with commercial distributions such as
       <a href="/wiki/Red_Hat_Enterprise_Linux" title="Red Hat Enterprise Linux">
        Red Hat Enterprise Linux
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SUSE_Linux_Enterprise_Server" title="SUSE Linux Enterprise Server">
        SUSE Linux Enterprise Server
       </a>
       . Distributions include the Linux kernel, supporting
       <a href="/wiki/System_software" title="System software">
        utilities
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Library_(computer_science)" title="Library (computer science)">
        libraries
       </a>
       , many of which are provided by the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_Project" title="GNU Project">
        GNU Project
       </a>
       , and usually a large amount of application software to fulfil the distribution's intended use. Desktop Linux distributions include a windowing system, such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X11" title="X11">
        X11
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mir_(software)" title="Mir (software)">
        Mir
       </a>
       or a
       <a href="/wiki/Wayland_(display_server_protocol)" title="Wayland (display server protocol)">
        Wayland
       </a>
       implementation, and an accompanying
       <a href="/wiki/Desktop_environment" title="Desktop environment">
        desktop environment
       </a>
       such as
       <a href="/wiki/GNOME" title="GNOME">
        GNOME
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/KDE_Plasma_5" title="KDE Plasma 5">
        KDE Plasma 5
       </a>
       ; some distributions may also include a less resource-intensive desktop, such as
       <a href="/wiki/LXDE" title="LXDE">
        LXDE
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Xfce" title="Xfce">
        Xfce
       </a>
       . Distributions intended to run on servers may omit all graphical environments from the standard install, and instead include other software to set up and operate a
       <a href="/wiki/Solution_stack" title="Solution stack">
        solution stack
       </a>
       such as
       <a href="/wiki/LAMP_(software_bundle)" title="LAMP (software bundle)">
        LAMP
       </a>
       . Because Linux is freely redistributable, anyone may create a distribution for any intended use. Many Linux distributions use the word "Linux" in their name. The
       <a href="/wiki/Free_Software_Foundation" title="Free Software Foundation">
        Free Software Foundation
       </a>
       uses the name "
       <a href="/wiki/GNU" title="GNU">
        GNU
       </a>
       /Linux" to refer to the operating system family, as well as specific distributions, to emphasize that most Linux distributions are not just the Linux kernel, and that they have in common not only the kernel, but also numerous utilities and libraries, a large proportion of which are from the GNU project. This has led to some
       <a href="/wiki/GNU/Linux_naming_controversy" title="GNU/Linux naming controversy">
        controversy
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gnu_linux_faq_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gnu_linux_faq-31">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-linux-and-gnu_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-linux-and-gnu-32">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Linux" title="History of Linux">
        History of Linux
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Precursors">
        Precursors
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Precursors">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linus_Torvalds_(cropped).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="298" data-file-width="234" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Linus_Torvalds_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Linus_Torvalds_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5c/Linus_Torvalds_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linus_Torvalds_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">
          Linus Torvalds
         </a>
         , principal author of the
         <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">
          Linux kernel
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">
        Unix
       </a>
       operating system was conceived and implemented in 1969, at
       <a href="/wiki/AT%26T" title="AT&amp;T">
        AT&amp;T
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bell_Laboratories" title="Bell Laboratories">
        Bell Laboratories
       </a>
       in the United States by
       <a href="/wiki/Ken_Thompson" title="Ken Thompson">
        Ken Thompson
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Ritchie" title="Dennis Ritchie">
        Dennis Ritchie
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Douglas_McIlroy" title="Douglas McIlroy">
        Douglas McIlroy
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Joe_Ossanna" title="Joe Ossanna">
        Joe Ossanna
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       First released in 1971, Unix was written entirely in
       <a href="/wiki/Assembly_language" title="Assembly language">
        assembly language
       </a>
       , as was common practice at the time. Later, in a key pioneering approach in 1973, it was rewritten in the
       <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">
        C
       </a>
       programming language by
       <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Ritchie" title="Dennis Ritchie">
        Dennis Ritchie
       </a>
       (with the exception of some hardware and I/O routines). The availability of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-level_language" title="High-level language">
        high-level language
       </a>
       implementation of Unix made its
       <a href="/wiki/Porting" title="Porting">
        porting
       </a>
       to different computer platforms easier.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Due to an earlier
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antitrust_case" title="Antitrust case">
        antitrust case
       </a>
       forbidding it from entering the computer business, AT&amp;T was required to license the operating system's source code to anyone who asked.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faqs.org_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-faqs.org-35">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result, Unix grew quickly and became widely adopted by academic institutions and businesses. In 1984, AT&amp;T divested itself of Bell Labs; freed of the legal obligation requiring free licensing, Bell Labs began selling Unix as a
       <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_software" title="Proprietary software">
        proprietary
       </a>
       product, where users weren't legally allowed to modify Unix. The
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_Project" title="GNU Project">
        GNU Project
       </a>
       , started in 1983 by
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Stallman" title="Richard Stallman">
        Richard Stallman
       </a>
       , had the goal of creating a "complete Unix-compatible software system" composed entirely of
       <a href="/wiki/Free_software" title="Free software">
        free software
       </a>
       . Work began in 1984.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gnu_announce_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gnu_announce-36">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Later, in 1985, Stallman started the
       <a href="/wiki/Free_Software_Foundation" title="Free Software Foundation">
        Free Software Foundation
       </a>
       and wrote the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_General_Public_License" title="GNU General Public License">
        GNU General Public License
       </a>
       (GNU GPL) in 1989. By the early 1990s, many of the programs required in an operating system (such as libraries,
       <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">
        compilers
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Text_editor" title="Text editor">
        text editors
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Unix_shell" title="Unix shell">
        Unix shell
       </a>
       , and a
       <a href="/wiki/Windowing_system" title="Windowing system">
        windowing system
       </a>
       ) were completed, although low-level elements such as
       <a href="/wiki/Device_driver" title="Device driver">
        device drivers
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daemon_(computer_software)" title="Daemon (computer software)">
        daemons
       </a>
       , and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernel_(computer_science)" title="Kernel (computer science)">
        kernel
       </a>
       , called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GNU/Hurd" title="GNU/Hurd">
        GNU/Hurd
       </a>
       , were stalled and incomplete.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Linus Torvalds has stated that if the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GNU_kernel" title="GNU kernel">
        GNU kernel
       </a>
       had been available at the time (1991), he would not have decided to write his own.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although not released until 1992, due to
       <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Software_Distribution#Net/2_and_legal_troubles" title="Berkeley Software Distribution">
        legal complications
       </a>
       , development of
       <a href="/wiki/386BSD" title="386BSD">
        386BSD
       </a>
       , from which
       <a href="/wiki/NetBSD" title="NetBSD">
        NetBSD
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/OpenBSD" title="OpenBSD">
        OpenBSD
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/FreeBSD" title="FreeBSD">
        FreeBSD
       </a>
       descended, predated that of Linux. Torvalds has also stated that if 386BSD had been available at the time, he probably would not have created Linux.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meta_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-meta-39">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/MINIX" title="MINIX">
        MINIX
       </a>
       was created by
       <a href="/wiki/Andrew_S._Tanenbaum" title="Andrew S. Tanenbaum">
        Andrew S. Tanenbaum
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">
        computer science
       </a>
       professor, and released in 1987 as a minimal
       <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">
        Unix-like
       </a>
       operating system targeted at students and others who wanted to learn the operating system principles. Although the complete source code of MINIX was freely available, the licensing terms prevented it from being
       <a href="/wiki/Free_software" title="Free software">
        free software
       </a>
       until the licensing changed in April 2000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minix-lic_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-minix-lic-40">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Creation">
        Creation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Creation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1991, while attending the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Helsinki" title="University of Helsinki">
        University of Helsinki
       </a>
       , Torvalds became curious about operating systems.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Frustrated by the licensing of MINIX, which at the time limited it to educational use only,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minix-lic_40-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-minix-lic-40">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       he began to work on his own operating system kernel, which eventually became the
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">
        Linux kernel
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Torvalds began the development of the Linux kernel on MINIX and applications written for MINIX were also used on Linux. Later, Linux matured and further Linux kernel development took place on Linux systems.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       GNU applications also replaced all MINIX components, because it was advantageous to use the freely available code from the GNU Project with the fledgling operating system; code licensed under the GNU GPL can be reused in other computer programs as long as they also are released under the same or a compatible license. Torvalds initiated a switch from his original license, which prohibited commercial redistribution, to the GNU GPL.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Developers worked to integrate GNU components with the Linux kernel, making a fully functional and free operating system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gnu_history_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gnu_history-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">
        <span id="FREAX">
        </span>
        Naming
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Naming">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linux_0_12.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1501" data-file-width="1517" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Linux_0_12.jpg/220px-Linux_0_12.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Linux_0_12.jpg/330px-Linux_0_12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Linux_0_12.jpg/440px-Linux_0_12.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linux_0_12.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         5.25-inch
         <a href="/wiki/Floppy_disk" title="Floppy disk">
          floppy disks
         </a>
         holding a very early version of Linux
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Linus Torvalds had wanted to call his invention "
       <b>
        Freax
       </b>
       ", a
       <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">
        portmanteau
       </a>
       of "free", "freak", and "x" (as an allusion to Unix). During the start of his work on the system, some of the project's
       <a href="/wiki/Makefile" title="Makefile">
        makefiles
       </a>
       included the name "Freax" for about half a year. Torvalds had already considered the name "Linux", but initially dismissed it as too egotistical.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fun_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-fun-45">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In order to facilitate development, the files were uploaded to the
       <a href="/wiki/File_Transfer_Protocol" title="File Transfer Protocol">
        FTP server
       </a>
       (ftp.funet.fi) of
       <a href="/wiki/FUNET" title="FUNET">
        FUNET
       </a>
       in September 1991.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ari_Lemmke" title="Ari Lemmke">
        Ari Lemmke
       </a>
       , Torvalds' coworker at the
       <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_University_of_Technology" title="Helsinki University of Technology">
        Helsinki University of Technology
       </a>
       (HUT), who was one of the volunteer administrators for the FTP server at the time, did not think that "Freax" was a good name. So, he named the project "Linux" on the server without consulting Torvalds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fun_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-fun-45">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Later, however, Torvalds consented to "Linux".
      </p>
      <p>
       To demonstrate how the word "Linux" should be pronounced (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="nowrap">
         (
         <span class="unicode haudio">
          <span class="fn">
           <span style="white-space:nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/File:Linus-linux.ogg" title="About this sound">
             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
            </a>
           </span>
           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/03/Linus-linux.ogg" title="Linus-linux.ogg">
            listen
           </a>
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          LIN
         </span>
         -əks
        </i>
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pronunciation-2_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-pronunciation-2-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foldoc09Jun06_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Foldoc09Jun06-12">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), Torvalds included an audio guide (
       <span class="unicode haudio">
        <span class="fn">
         <span style="white-space:nowrap">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Linus-linux.ogg" title="About this sound">

          </a>

         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">
         (
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">

          </span>
         </a>
         ·
         <a href="/wiki/File:Linus-linux.ogg" title="File:Linus-linux.ogg">
          <span style="cursor:help;">

          </span>
         </a>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       ) with the kernel source code.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pronounce_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pronounce-46">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Another variant of pronunciation is
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">

           </span>

           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          LYN
         </span>
         -əks
        </i>
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foldoc09Jun06_12-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Foldoc09Jun06-12">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_and_popular_uptake">
        Commercial and popular uptake
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Commercial and popular uptake">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_adoption" title="Linux adoption">
        Linux adoption
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ubuntu_15.10_with_Firefox_and_Nautilus_open.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="737" data-file-width="1279" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Ubuntu_15.10_with_Firefox_and_Nautilus_open.png/220px-Ubuntu_15.10_with_Firefox_and_Nautilus_open.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Ubuntu_15.10_with_Firefox_and_Nautilus_open.png/330px-Ubuntu_15.10_with_Firefox_and_Nautilus_open.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Ubuntu_15.10_with_Firefox_and_Nautilus_open.png/440px-Ubuntu_15.10_with_Firefox_and_Nautilus_open.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ubuntu_15.10_with_Firefox_and_Nautilus_open.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_(operating_system)" title="Ubuntu (operating system)">
          Ubuntu
         </a>
         , a popular Linux distribution
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nexus_5X_(White).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1372" data-file-width="1160" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Nexus_5X_%28White%29.jpg/220px-Nexus_5X_%28White%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Nexus_5X_%28White%29.jpg/330px-Nexus_5X_%28White%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Nexus_5X_%28White%29.jpg/440px-Nexus_5X_%28White%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nexus_5X_(White).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Nexus_5X" title="Nexus 5X">
          Nexus 5X
         </a>
         running
         <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">
          Android
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Adoption of Linux in production environments, rather than being used only by hobbyists, started to take off first in the mid-1990s in the supercomputing community, where organizations such as
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       started to replace their increasingly expensive machines with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluster_computing" title="Cluster computing">
        clusters
       </a>
       of inexpensive commodity computers running Linux. Commercial use followed when
       <a href="/wiki/Dell" title="Dell">
        Dell
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">
        IBM
       </a>
       , followed by
       <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">
        Hewlett-Packard
       </a>
       , started offering Linux support to escape
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">
        Microsoft
       </a>
       's monopoly in the desktop operating system market.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-security_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-security-47">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Today, Linux systems are used throughout computing, from
       <a href="/wiki/Embedded_system" title="Embedded system">
        embedded systems
       </a>
       to virtually all
       <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer" title="Supercomputer">
        supercomputers
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rules_supercomputers_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rules_supercomputers-20">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and have secured a place in
       <a href="/wiki/Server_(computing)" title="Server (computing)">
        server
       </a>
       installations such as the popular
       <a href="/wiki/LAMP_(software_bundle)" title="LAMP (software bundle)">
        LAMP
       </a>
       application stack.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Use of Linux distributions in home and enterprise desktops has been growing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galli2007_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-galli2007-50">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paul2007_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-paul2007-51">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beer2007_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-beer2007-52">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-applications2007_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-applications2007-53">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xitimonitor2007_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-xitimonitor2007-54">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-globalstats2007_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-globalstats2007-55">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeitgeist2004_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-zeitgeist2004-56">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Linux distributions have also become popular in the
       <a href="/wiki/Netbook" title="Netbook">
        netbook
       </a>
       market, with many devices shipping with customized Linux distributions installed, and Google releasing their own
       <a href="/wiki/Chrome_OS" title="Chrome OS">
        Chrome OS
       </a>
       designed for netbooks.
      </p>
      <p>
       Linux's greatest success in the consumer market is perhaps the mobile device market, with
       <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">
        Android
       </a>
       being one of the most dominant operating systems on
       <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">
        smartphones
       </a>
       and very popular on
       <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">
        tablets
       </a>
       and, more recently, on
       <a href="/wiki/Wearable_technology" title="Wearable technology">
        wearables
       </a>
       . Linux gaming is also on the rise with
       <a href="/wiki/Valve_Corporation" title="Valve Corporation">
        Valve
       </a>
       showing its support for Linux and rolling out its own gaming oriented Linux distribution. Linux distributions have also gained popularity with various local and national governments, such as the federal government of
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Current_development">
        Current development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Current development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:In_flight_system_Linux_bootup.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/In_flight_system_Linux_bootup.jpg/220px-In_flight_system_Linux_bootup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/In_flight_system_Linux_bootup.jpg/330px-In_flight_system_Linux_bootup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/In_flight_system_Linux_bootup.jpg/440px-In_flight_system_Linux_bootup.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:In_flight_system_Linux_bootup.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         In flight entertainment system booting up showing the Linux logo
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Torvalds continues to direct the development of the kernel.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Stallman heads the Free Software Foundation,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which in turn supports the GNU components.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Finally, individuals and corporations develop third-party non-GNU components. These third-party components comprise a vast body of work and may include both kernel modules and user applications and libraries.
      </p>
      <p>
       Linux vendors and communities combine and distribute the kernel, GNU components, and non-GNU components, with additional
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Package_management" title="Package management">
        package management
       </a>
       software in the form of
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_distribution" title="Linux distribution">
        Linux distributions
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Design">
        Design
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Design">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       A Linux-based system is a modular
       <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">
        Unix-like
       </a>
       operating system, deriving much of its basic design from principles established in Unix during the 1970s and 1980s. Such a system uses a
       <a href="/wiki/Monolithic_kernel" title="Monolithic kernel">
        monolithic kernel
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">
        Linux kernel
       </a>
       , which handles process control, networking, access to the
       <a href="/wiki/Peripheral" title="Peripheral">
        peripherals
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/File_system" title="File system">
        file systems
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Device_drivers" title="Device drivers">
        Device drivers
       </a>
       are either integrated directly with the kernel, or added as modules that are loaded while the system is running.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The GNU
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Userland_(computing)" title="Userland (computing)">
        userland
       </a>
       is a key part of most systems based on the Linux kernel, with Android being the notable exception. The Project's
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_C_Library" title="GNU C Library">
        implementation
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/C_standard_library" title="C standard library">
        C library
       </a>
       functions as a wrapper for the system calls of the Linux kernel necessary to the kernel-userspace interface, the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_toolchain" title="GNU toolchain">
        toolchain
       </a>
       is a broad collection of programming tools vital to Linux development (including the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_Compiler_Collection" title="GNU Compiler Collection">
        compilers
       </a>
       used to build the Linux kernel itself), and the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_Core_Utilities" title="GNU Core Utilities">
        coreutils
       </a>
       implement many basic
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unix_commands" title="Unix commands">
        Unix tools
       </a>
       . The project also develops
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bash_(shell)" title="Bash (shell)">
        a popular
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Command-line_interface" title="Command-line interface">
        CLI
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Shell_(computing)" title="Shell (computing)">
        shell
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">
        graphical user interface
       </a>
       (or GUI) used by most Linux systems is built on top of an implementation of the
       <a href="/wiki/X_Window_System" title="X Window System">
        X Window System
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oreilly-anatomy_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-oreilly-anatomy-62">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       More recently, the Linux community seeks to advance to
       <a href="/wiki/Wayland_(display_server_protocol)" title="Wayland (display server protocol)">
        Wayland
       </a>
       as the new display server protocol in place of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X11" title="X11">
        X11
       </a>
       . Many other open-source software projects contribute to Linux systems.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        Various layers within Linux, also showing separation between the
        <a href="/wiki/User_space" title="User space">
         userland
        </a>

         <br/>
         <i>

         </i>

         <i>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMD_Catalyst" title="AMD Catalyst">

          </a>
         </i>
         , ...
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background-color: #96d5aa">
        <td colspan="1" style="width: 10%">
         <b>
          <a href="/wiki/C_standard_library" title="C standard library">
           C standard library
          </a>
         </b>
        </td>
        <td colspan="5" style="width: 80%">
         <tt>
          open()
         </tt>
         ,
         <tt>
          exec()
         </tt>
         ,
         <tt>
          sbrk()
         </tt>
         ,
         <tt>
          socket()
         </tt>
         ,
         <tt>
          fopen()
         </tt>
         ,
         <tt>
          calloc()
         </tt>
         , ... (up to 2000
         <a href="/wiki/Subroutine" title="Subroutine">
          subroutines
         </a>
         )
         <br/>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/GNU_C_Library" title="GNU C Library">
           glibc
          </a>
         </i>
         aims to be
         <a href="/wiki/POSIX" title="POSIX">
          POSIX
         </a>
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Single_UNIX_Specification" title="Single UNIX Specification">
          SUS
         </a>
         -compatible,
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/UClibc" title="UClibc">
           uClibc
          </a>
         </i>
         targets embedded systems,
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Bionic_(software)" title="Bionic (software)">
           bionic
          </a>
         </i>
         written for
         <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">
          Android
         </a>
         , etc.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th rowspan="3" style="width: 10%">
         Kernel mode
        </th>
        <td rowspan="3" style="width: 10%">
         <b>
          <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">
           Linux kernel
          </a>
         </b>
        </td>
        <td colspan="5" style="width: 80%; background-color: #ffb7b7;">
         <tt>
          <a href="/wiki/Stat_(system_call)" title="Stat (system call)">

          </a>
         </tt>
         ,
         <tt>
          <a href="/wiki/Splice_(system_call)" title="Splice (system call)">
           splice
          </a>
         </tt>
         ,
         <tt>
          <a href="/wiki/Dup_(system_call)" title="Dup (system call)">
           dup
          </a>
         </tt>
         ,
         <tt>
          <a href="/wiki/Read_(system_call)" title="Read (system call)">
           read
          </a>
         </tt>
         ,
         <tt>
          <a href="/wiki/Open_(system_call)" title="Open (system call)">
           open
          </a>
         </tt>
         ,
         <tt>
          <a href="/wiki/Ioctl" title="Ioctl">

          </a>
         </tt>

          </a>

         <tt>

          </a>

         , etc. (about 380 system calls)
         <br/>
         The Linux kernel
         <a href="/wiki/System_call" title="System call">
          System Call Interface
         </a>
         (SCI, aims to be
         <a href="/wiki/POSIX" title="POSIX">
          POSIX
         </a>
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Single_UNIX_Specification" title="Single UNIX Specification">
          SUS
         </a>
         -compatible)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background-color: #ffb7b7;">
        <td style="width: 18%;">

         </a>
         <br/>
         subsystem
        </td>

         <br/>
         subsystem
        </td>

         subsystem
        </td>

         <br/>
         subsystem
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="background-color: #ffb7b7;">
        <td colspan="5" style="width: 80%;">
         Other components:
         <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Linux_Sound_Architecture" title="Advanced Linux Sound Architecture">
          ALSA
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Direct_Rendering_Infrastructure" title="Direct Rendering Infrastructure">
          DRI
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Evdev" title="Evdev">
          evdev
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Logical_Volume_Manager_(Linux)" title="Logical Volume Manager (Linux)">
          LVM
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Device_mapper" title="Device mapper">
          device mapper
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linux_Network_Scheduler" title="Linux Network Scheduler">
          Linux Network Scheduler
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Netfilter" title="Netfilter">
          Netfilter
         </a>
         <br/>
         <a href="/wiki/Linux_Security_Modules" title="Linux Security Modules">
          Linux Security Modules
         </a>
         :
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Security-Enhanced_Linux" title="Security-Enhanced Linux">

          </a>
         </i>

         <i>

          </a>

          </a>

          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smack_(Linux_security_module)" title="Smack (Linux security module)">

          </a>

       </tr>
       <tr style="background-color: #ffd99c;">
        <th colspan="7" style="width: 100%">
         Hardware (
         <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">
          CPU
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Random-access_memory" title="Random-access memory">
          main memory
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Computer_data_storage" title="Computer data storage">
          data storage devices
         </a>
         , etc.)
        </th>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Installed components of a Linux system include the following:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oreilly-anatomy_62-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-oreilly-anatomy-62">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        A
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bootloader" title="Bootloader">
         bootloader
        </a>
        , for example
        <a href="/wiki/GNU_GRUB" title="GNU GRUB">
         GNU GRUB
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/LILO_(boot_loader)" title="LILO (boot loader)">
         LILO
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/SYSLINUX" title="SYSLINUX">
         SYSLINUX
        </a>
        , or
        <a href="/wiki/Gummiboot_(software)" title="Gummiboot (software)">
         Gummiboot
        </a>
        . This is a program that loads the Linux kernel into the computer's
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main_memory" title="Main memory">
         main memory
        </a>
        , by being executed by the computer when it is turned on and after the
        <a href="/wiki/Firmware" title="Firmware">
         firmware
        </a>
        initialization is performed.
       </li>
       <li>
        An
        <a href="/wiki/Init" title="Init">
         init
        </a>
        program, such as the traditional
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sysvinit" title="Sysvinit">
         sysvinit
        </a>
        and the newer
        <a href="/wiki/Systemd" title="Systemd">
         systemd
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/OpenRC" title="OpenRC">
         OpenRC
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upstart_(software)" title="Upstart (software)">
         Upstart
        </a>
        . This is the first
        <a href="/wiki/Process_(computing)" title="Process (computing)">
         process
        </a>
        launched by the Linux kernel, and is at the root of the process tree: in other terms, all processes are launched through init. It starts processes such as system services and login prompts (whether graphical or in terminal mode).
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Library_(computing)" title="Library (computing)">
         Software libraries
        </a>
        , which contain code that can be used by running processes. On Linux systems using
        <a href="/wiki/Executable_and_Linkable_Format" title="Executable and Linkable Format">
         ELF
        </a>
        -format executable files, the
        <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_linker" title="Dynamic linker">
         dynamic linker
        </a>
        that manages use of dynamic libraries is known as
        <tt>
         ld-linux.so
        </tt>
        . If the system is set up for the user to compile software themselves,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Header_file" title="Header file">
         header files
        </a>
        will also be included to describe the
        <a href="/wiki/Application_binary_interface" title="Application binary interface">
         interface
        </a>
        of installed libraries. Besides the most commonly used software library on Linux systems, the
        <a href="/wiki/GNU_C_Library" title="GNU C Library">
         GNU C Library
        </a>
        (glibc), there are numerous other libraries, such as
        <a href="/wiki/Simple_DirectMedia_Layer" title="Simple DirectMedia Layer">
         SDL
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Mesa_(computer_graphics)" title="Mesa (computer graphics)">
         Mesa
        </a>
        .
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/C_standard_library" title="C standard library">
           C standard library
          </a>
          is the library needed to run
          <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">
           C programs
          </a>
          on a computer system, with the GNU C Library being the standard. For embedded systems, alternatives such as the
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EGLIBC" title="EGLIBC">
           EGLIBC
          </a>
          (a glibc fork once used by Debian) and
          <a href="/wiki/UClibc" title="UClibc">
           uClibc
          </a>
          (which was designed for
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UClinux" title="UClinux">
           uClinux
          </a>
          ) have been developed, although both are no longer maintained. Android uses its own C library,
          <a href="/wiki/Bionic_(software)" title="Bionic (software)">
           Bionic
          </a>
          .
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        Basic Unix commands, with GNU coreutils being the standard implementation. Alternatives exist for embedded systems, such as the copyleft
        <a href="/wiki/BusyBox" title="BusyBox">
         BusyBox
        </a>
        , and the BSD-licensed
        <a href="/wiki/Toybox" title="Toybox">
         Toybox
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Widget_toolkit" title="Widget toolkit">
         Widget toolkits
        </a>
        are the libraries used to build
        <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">
         graphical user interfaces
        </a>
        (GUIs) for software applications. Numerous widget toolkits are available, including
        <a href="/wiki/GTK%2B" title="GTK+">
         GTK+
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Clutter_(software)" title="Clutter (software)">
         Clutter
        </a>
        developed by the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GNOME_project" title="GNOME project">
         GNOME project
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Qt_(software)" title="Qt (software)">
         Qt
        </a>
        developed by the
        <a href="/wiki/Qt_Project" title="Qt Project">
         Qt Project
        </a>
        and led by
        <a href="/wiki/Digia" title="Digia">
         Digia
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_Foundation_Libraries" title="Enlightenment Foundation Libraries">
         Enlightenment Foundation Libraries
        </a>
        (EFL) developed primarily by the
        <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_(software)" title="Enlightenment (software)">
         Enlightenment
        </a>
        team.
       </li>
       <li>
        A
        <a href="/wiki/Package_manager" title="Package manager">
         package management system
        </a>
        , such as
        <a href="/wiki/Dpkg" title="Dpkg">
         dpkg
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RPM_Package_Manager" title="RPM Package Manager">
         RPM
        </a>
        . Alternatively packages can be compiled from binary or source
        <a href="/wiki/Tar_(computing)" title="Tar (computing)">
         tarballs
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        User interface programs such as command shells or windowing environments.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="User_interface">
        User interface
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: User interface">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/User_interface" title="User interface">
        user interface
       </a>
       , also known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Shell_(computing)" title="Shell (computing)">
        shell
       </a>
       , is either a
       <a href="/wiki/Command-line_interface" title="Command-line interface">
        command-line interface
       </a>
       (CLI), a
       <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">
        graphical user interface
       </a>
       (GUI), or through controls attached to the associated hardware, which is common for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embedded_systems" title="Embedded systems">
        embedded systems
       </a>
       . For desktop systems, the default mode is usually a graphical user interface, although the CLI is commonly available through
       <a href="/wiki/Terminal_emulator" title="Terminal emulator">
        terminal emulator
       </a>
       windows or on a separate
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtual_console_(PC)" title="Virtual console (PC)">
        virtual console
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       CLI shells are text-based user interfaces, which use text for both input and output. The dominant shell used in Linux is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bourne-Again_Shell" title="Bourne-Again Shell">
        Bourne-Again Shell
       </a>
       (bash), originally developed for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GNU_project" title="GNU project">
        GNU project
       </a>
       . Most low-level Linux components, including various parts of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Userland_(computing)" title="Userland (computing)">
        userland
       </a>
       , use the CLI exclusively. The CLI is particularly suited for automation of repetitive or delayed tasks, and provides very simple
       <a href="/wiki/Inter-process_communication" title="Inter-process communication">
        inter-process communication
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       On desktop systems, the most popular user interfaces are the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GUI_shell" title="GUI shell">
        GUI shells
       </a>
       , packaged together with extensive
       <a href="/wiki/Desktop_environment" title="Desktop environment">
        desktop environments
       </a>
       , such as the
       <a href="/wiki/KDE" title="KDE">
        K Desktop Environment (KDE)
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GNOME_desktop" title="GNOME desktop">
        GNOME
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/MATE_(software)" title="MATE (software)">
        MATE
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cinnamon_(desktop_environment)" title="Cinnamon (desktop environment)">
        Cinnamon
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unity_(desktop_environment)" title="Unity (desktop environment)">
        Unity
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/LXDE" title="LXDE">
        LXDE
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Elementary_OS" title="Elementary OS">
        Pantheon
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Xfce" title="Xfce">
        Xfce
       </a>
       , though a variety of additional user interfaces exist. Most popular user interfaces are based on the
       <a href="/wiki/X_Window_System" title="X Window System">
        X Window System
       </a>
       , often simply called "X". It provides
       <a href="/wiki/Network_transparency" title="Network transparency">
        network transparency
       </a>
       and permits a graphical application running on one system to be displayed on another where a user may interact with the application; however, certain extensions of the X Window System are not capable of working over the network.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Several X display servers exist, with the reference implementation,
       <a href="/wiki/X.Org_Server" title="X.Org Server">
        X.Org Server
       </a>
       , being the most popular.
      </p>
      <p>
       Several types of
       <a href="/wiki/Window_manager" title="Window manager">
        window managers
       </a>
       exist for X11, including
       <a href="/wiki/Tiling_window_manager" title="Tiling window manager">
        tiling
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_window_manager" title="Dynamic window manager">
        dynamic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stacking_window_manager" title="Stacking window manager">
        stacking
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Compositing_window_manager" title="Compositing window manager">
        compositing
       </a>
       . Window managers provide means to control the placement and appearance of individual application windows, and interact with the X Window System. Simpler
       <a href="/wiki/X_window_manager" title="X window manager">
        X window managers
       </a>
       such as
       <a href="/wiki/Dwm" title="Dwm">
        dwm
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Ratpoison" title="Ratpoison">
        ratpoison
       </a>
       provide a
       <a href="/wiki/Minimalism_(computing)" title="Minimalism (computing)">
        minimalist
       </a>
       functionality, while more elaborate window managers such as
       <a href="/wiki/FVWM" title="FVWM">
        FVWM
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_(software)" title="Enlightenment (software)">
        Enlightenment
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Window_Maker" title="Window Maker">
        Window Maker
       </a>
       provide more features such as a built-in
       <a href="/wiki/Taskbar" title="Taskbar">
        taskbar
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Theme_(computing)" title="Theme (computing)">
        themes
       </a>
       , but are still lightweight when compared to desktop environments. Desktop environments include window managers as part of their standard installations, such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutter_(window_manager)" title="Mutter (window manager)">
        Mutter
       </a>
       (GNOME),
       <a href="/wiki/KWin" title="KWin">
        KWin
       </a>
       (KDE) or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xfwm" title="Xfwm">
        Xfwm
       </a>
       (xfce), although users may choose to use a different window manager if preferred.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Wayland_(display_server_protocol)" title="Wayland (display server protocol)">
        Wayland
       </a>
       is a display server protocol intended as a replacement for the X11 protocol; as of 2014
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , it has not received wider adoption. Unlike X11, Wayland does not need an external window manager and compositing manager. Therefore, a Wayland compositor takes the role of the display server, window manager and compositing manager. Weston is the reference implementation of Wayland, while GNOME's Mutter and KDE's KWin are being ported to Wayland as standalone display servers. Enlightenment has already been successfully ported since version 19.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Video_input_infrastructure">
        Video input infrastructure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Video input infrastructure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Video4Linux" title="Video4Linux">
        Video4Linux
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Linux currently has two modern kernel-userspace APIs for handling video input devices:
       <a href="/wiki/Video4Linux" title="Video4Linux">
        V4L2
       </a>
       API for video streams and radio, and
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_Video_Broadcasting" title="Digital Video Broadcasting">
        DVB
       </a>
       API for digital TV reception.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Due to the complexity and diversity of different devices, and due to the large amount of formats and standards handled by those APIs, this infrastructure needs to evolve to better fit other devices. Also, a good userspace device library is the key of the success for having userspace applications to be able to work with all formats supported by those devices.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Development">
        Development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">

          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">

          <span class="hide-when-compact">

          </span>

          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">

         </div>
