      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unix_timeline.en.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="870" height="379" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Unix_timeline.en.svg/500px-Unix_timeline.en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Unix_timeline.en.svg/750px-Unix_timeline.en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Unix_timeline.en.svg/1000px-Unix_timeline.en.svg.png 2x" width="500"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unix_timeline.en.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Simplified history of
         <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">
          Unix-like
         </a>
         operating systems. Linux shares similar architecture and concepts (as part of the
         <a href="/wiki/POSIX" title="POSIX">
          POSIX
         </a>
         standard) but does not share non-free source code with the original
         <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">
          Unix
         </a>
         or
         <a href="/wiki/MINIX" title="MINIX">
          MINIX
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_distribution" title="Linux distribution">
        Linux distribution
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Free_software" title="Free software">
        Free software
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The primary difference between Linux and many other popular contemporary operating systems is that the
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">
        Linux kernel
       </a>
       and other components are
       <a href="/wiki/Free_software" title="Free software">
        free
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">
        open-source software
       </a>
       . Linux is not the only such operating system, although it is by far the most widely used.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarketShare09NOV_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MarketShare09NOV-68">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some
       <a href="/wiki/Free_software_license" title="Free software license">
        free
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Open-source_license" title="Open-source license">
        open-source
       </a>
       software licenses are based on the principle of
       <a href="/wiki/Copyleft" title="Copyleft">
        copyleft
       </a>
       , a kind of reciprocity: any work derived from a copyleft piece of software must also be copyleft itself. The most common free software license, the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_General_Public_License" title="GNU General Public License">
        GNU General Public License
       </a>
       (GPL), is a form of copyleft, and is used for the Linux kernel and many of the components from the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_Project" title="GNU Project">
        GNU Project
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Linux based distributions are intended by developers for
       <a href="/wiki/Interoperability" title="Interoperability">
        interoperability
       </a>
       with other operating systems and established computing standards. Linux systems adhere to
       <a href="/wiki/POSIX" title="POSIX">
        POSIX
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Single_UNIX_Specification" title="Single UNIX Specification">
        SUS
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_Standard_Base" title="Linux Standard Base">
        LSB
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">
        ISO
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/American_National_Standards_Institute" title="American National Standards Institute">
        ANSI
       </a>
       standards where possible, although to date only one Linux distribution has been POSIX.1 certified, Linux-FT.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Free software projects, although developed through
       <a href="/wiki/Collaboration" title="Collaboration">
        collaboration
       </a>
       , are often produced independently of each other. The fact that the software licenses explicitly permit redistribution, however, provides a basis for larger scale projects that collect the software produced by stand-alone projects and make it available all at once in the form of a Linux distribution.
      </p>
      <p>
       Many Linux distributions, or "distros", manage a remote collection of system software and application software packages available for download and installation through a network connection. This allows users to adapt the operating system to their specific needs. Distributions are maintained by individuals, loose-knit teams, volunteer organizations, and commercial entities. A distribution is responsible for the default configuration of the installed Linux kernel, general system security, and more generally integration of the different software packages into a coherent whole. Distributions typically use a
       <a href="/wiki/Package_manager" title="Package manager">
        package manager
       </a>
       such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_Packaging_Tool" title="Advanced Packaging Tool">
        apt
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yellowdog_Updater,_Modified" title="Yellowdog Updater, Modified">
        yum
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zypper" title="Zypper">
        zypper
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacman_(package_manager)" title="Pacman (package manager)">
        pacman
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Portage_(software)" title="Portage (software)">
        portage
       </a>
       to install, remove, and update all of a system's software from one central location.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Community">
        Community
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Community">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_software_community" title="Free software community">
        Free software community
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linux_User_Group" title="Linux User Group">
        Linux User Group
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       A distribution is largely driven by its developer and user communities. Some vendors develop and fund their distributions on a volunteer basis,
       <a href="/wiki/Debian" title="Debian">
        Debian
       </a>
       being a well-known example. Others maintain a community version of their commercial distributions, as
       <a href="/wiki/Red_Hat" title="Red Hat">
        Red Hat
       </a>
       does with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fedora_(Linux_distribution)" title="Fedora (Linux distribution)">
        Fedora
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/SUSE" title="SUSE">
        SUSE
       </a>
       does with
       <a href="/wiki/OpenSUSE" title="OpenSUSE">
        openSUSE
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In many cities and regions, local associations known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linux_User_Group" title="Linux User Group">
        Linux User Groups
       </a>
       (LUGs) seek to promote their preferred distribution and by extension free software. They hold meetings and provide free demonstrations, training, technical support, and operating system installation to new users. Many Internet communities also provide support to Linux users and developers. Most distributions and free software / open-source projects have
       <a href="/wiki/Internet_Relay_Chat" title="Internet Relay Chat">
        IRC
       </a>
       chatrooms or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newsgroup" title="Newsgroup">
        newsgroups
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Online_forum" title="Online forum">
        Online forums
       </a>
       are another means for support, with notable examples being
       <a href="/wiki/LinuxQuestions.org" title="LinuxQuestions.org">
        LinuxQuestions.org
       </a>
       and the various distribution specific support and community forums, such as ones for
       <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_(operating_system)" title="Ubuntu (operating system)">
        Ubuntu
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fedora_(operating_system)" title="Fedora (operating system)">
        Fedora
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Gentoo_Linux" title="Gentoo Linux">
        Gentoo
       </a>
       . Linux distributions host
       <a href="/wiki/Mailing_list" title="Mailing list">
        mailing lists
       </a>
       ; commonly there will be a specific topic such as usage or development for a given list.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are several technology websites with a Linux focus. Print magazines on Linux often bundle
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cover_disk" title="Cover disk">
        cover disks
       </a>
       that carry software or even complete Linux distributions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although Linux distributions are generally available without charge, several large corporations sell, support, and contribute to the development of the components of the system and of
       <a href="/wiki/Free_software" title="Free software">
        free software
       </a>
       . An analysis of the Linux kernel showed 75 percent of the code from December 2008 to January 2010 was developed by programmers working for corporations, leaving about 18 percent to volunteers and 7% unclassified.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Major corporations that provide contributions include
       <a href="/wiki/Dell" title="Dell">
        Dell
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">
        IBM
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">
        HP
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oracle_Corporation" title="Oracle Corporation">
        Oracle
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sun_Microsystems" title="Sun Microsystems">
        Sun Microsystems
       </a>
       (now part of Oracle) and
       <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">
        Nokia
       </a>
       . A number of corporations, notably Red Hat,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canonical_Ltd." title="Canonical Ltd.">
        Canonical
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/SUSE" title="SUSE">
        SUSE
       </a>
       , have built a significant business around Linux distributions.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_software_licenses" title="Free software licenses">
        free software licenses
       </a>
       , on which the various software packages of a distribution built on the Linux kernel are based, explicitly accommodate and encourage commercialization; the relationship between a Linux distribution as a whole and individual vendors may be seen as
       <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">
        symbiotic
       </a>
       . One common
       <a href="/wiki/Business_model" title="Business model">
        business model
       </a>
       of commercial suppliers is charging for support, especially for business users. A number of companies also offer a specialized business version of their distribution, which adds proprietary support packages and tools to administer higher numbers of installations or to simplify administrative tasks.
      </p>
      <p>
       Another business model is to give away the software in order to sell hardware. This used to be the norm in the computer industry, with operating systems such as
       <a href="/wiki/CP/M" title="CP/M">
        CP/M
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Apple_DOS" title="Apple DOS">
        Apple DOS
       </a>
       and versions of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mac_OS" title="Mac OS">
        Mac OS
       </a>
       prior to 7.6 freely copyable (but not modifiable). As computer hardware standardized throughout the 1980s, it became more difficult for hardware manufacturers to profit from this tactic, as the OS would run on any manufacturer's computer that shared the same architecture.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Programming_on_Linux">
        Programming on Linux
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Programming on Linux">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Linux distributions support dozens of
       <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">
        programming languages
       </a>
       . The original development tools used for building both Linux applications and operating system programs are found within the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_toolchain" title="GNU toolchain">
        GNU toolchain
       </a>
       , which includes the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_Compiler_Collection" title="GNU Compiler Collection">
        GNU Compiler Collection
       </a>
       (GCC) and the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_Build_System" title="GNU Build System">
        GNU Build System
       </a>
       . Amongst others, GCC provides compilers for
       <a href="/wiki/Ada_(programming_language)" title="Ada (programming language)">
        Ada
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">
        C
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">
        C++
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Go_(programming_language)" title="Go (programming language)">
        Go
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fortran" title="Fortran">
        Fortran
       </a>
       . Many programming languages have a cross-platform reference implementation that supports Linux, for example
       <a href="/wiki/PHP" title="PHP">
        PHP
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">
        Perl
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruby_programming_language" title="Ruby programming language">
        Ruby
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Python_programming_language" title="Python programming language">
        Python
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">
        Java
       </a>
       , Go,
       <a href="/wiki/Rust_(programming_language)" title="Rust (programming language)">
        Rust
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Haskell_(programming_language)" title="Haskell (programming language)">
        Haskell
       </a>
       . First released in 2003, the
       <a href="/wiki/LLVM" title="LLVM">
        LLVM
       </a>
       project provides an alternative cross-platform open-source compiler for many languages.
       <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_software" title="Proprietary software">
        Proprietary
       </a>
       compilers for Linux include the
       <a href="/wiki/Intel_C%2B%2B_Compiler" title="Intel C++ Compiler">
        Intel C++ Compiler
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun_Studio_(software)" title="Sun Studio (software)">
        Sun Studio
       </a>
       , and IBM XL
       <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">
        C
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">
        C++
       </a>
       Compiler.
       <a href="/wiki/BASIC" title="BASIC">
        BASIC
       </a>
       in the form of
       <a href="/wiki/Visual_Basic" title="Visual Basic">
        Visual Basic
       </a>
       is supported in such forms as
       <a href="/wiki/Gambas" title="Gambas">
        Gambas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/FreeBASIC" title="FreeBASIC">
        FreeBASIC
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/XBasic" title="XBasic">
        XBasic
       </a>
       , and in terms of terminal programming or
       <a href="/wiki/QuickBASIC" title="QuickBASIC">
        QuickBASIC
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbo_BASIC" title="Turbo BASIC">
        Turbo BASIC
       </a>
       programming in the form of
       <a href="/wiki/QB64" title="QB64">
        QB64
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A common feature of Unix-like systems, Linux includes traditional specific-purpose programming languages targeted at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Script_(computing)" title="Script (computing)">
        scripting
       </a>
       , text processing and system configuration and management in general. Linux distributions support
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shell_scripts" title="Shell scripts">
        shell scripts
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Awk" title="Awk">
        awk
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sed" title="Sed">
        sed
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Make_(software)" title="Make (software)">
        make
       </a>
       . Many programs also have an embedded programming language to support configuring or programming themselves. For example,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regular_expressions" title="Regular expressions">
        regular expressions
       </a>
       are supported in programs like
       <a href="/wiki/Grep" title="Grep">
        grep
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Locate_(Unix)" title="Locate (Unix)">
        locate
       </a>
       , the traditional Unix
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mail_Transfer_Agent" title="Mail Transfer Agent">
        MTA
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Sendmail" title="Sendmail">
        Sendmail
       </a>
       contains its own
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turing_complete" title="Turing complete">
        Turing complete
       </a>
       scripting system, and the advanced text editor
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_Emacs" title="GNU Emacs">
        GNU Emacs
       </a>
       is built around a general purpose
       <a href="/wiki/Emacs_Lisp" title="Emacs Lisp">
        Lisp
       </a>
       interpreter.
      </p>
      <p>
       Most distributions also include support for
       <a href="/wiki/PHP" title="PHP">
        PHP
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">
        Perl
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruby_programming_language" title="Ruby programming language">
        Ruby
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Python_programming_language" title="Python programming language">
        Python
       </a>
       and other
       <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_programming_language" title="Dynamic programming language">
        dynamic languages
       </a>
       . While not as common, Linux also supports
       <a href="/wiki/C_Sharp_(programming_language)" title="C Sharp (programming language)">
        C#
       </a>
       (via
       <a href="/wiki/Mono_(software)" title="Mono (software)">
        Mono
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Vala_(programming_language)" title="Vala (programming language)">
        Vala
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Scheme_(programming_language)" title="Scheme (programming language)">
        Scheme
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_Guile#Guile_Scheme" title="GNU Guile">
        Guile Scheme
       </a>
       acts as an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extension_language" title="Extension language">
        extension language
       </a>
       targeting the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU" title="GNU">
        GNU
       </a>
       system utilities, seeking to make the conventionally small,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Static_typing" title="Static typing">
        static
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">
        compiled
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">
        C
       </a>
       programs of
       <a href="/wiki/Unix_philosophy" title="Unix philosophy">
        Unix design
       </a>
       rapidly and dynamically extensible via an elegant,
       <a href="/wiki/Functional_programming" title="Functional programming">
        functional
       </a>
       high-level scripting system; many GNU programs can be compiled with optional Guile
       <a href="/wiki/Language_binding" title="Language binding">
        bindings
       </a>
       to this end. A number of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Java_Virtual_Machine" title="Java Virtual Machine">
        Java Virtual Machines
       </a>
       and development kits run on Linux, including the original Sun Microsystems JVM (
       <a href="/wiki/HotSpot" title="HotSpot">
        HotSpot
       </a>
       ), and IBM's J2SE RE, as well as many open-source projects like
       <a href="/wiki/Kaffe" title="Kaffe">
        Kaffe
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JikesRVM" title="JikesRVM">
        JikesRVM
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/GNOME" title="GNOME">
        GNOME
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/KDE" title="KDE">
        KDE
       </a>
       are popular
       <a href="/wiki/Desktop_environment" title="Desktop environment">
        desktop environments
       </a>
       and provide a framework for developing applications. These projects are based on the
       <a href="/wiki/GTK%2B" title="GTK+">
        GTK+
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qt_(toolkit)" title="Qt (toolkit)">
        Qt
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Widget_toolkit" title="Widget toolkit">
        widget toolkits
       </a>
       , respectively, which can also be used independently of the larger framework. Both support a wide variety of languages. There are a number of
       <a href="/wiki/Integrated_development_environment" title="Integrated development environment">
        Integrated development environments
       </a>
       available including
       <a href="/wiki/Anjuta" title="Anjuta">
        Anjuta
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Code::Blocks" title="Code::Blocks">
        Code::Blocks
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/CodeLite" title="CodeLite">
        CodeLite
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_(software)" title="Eclipse (software)">
        Eclipse
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Geany" title="Geany">
        Geany
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ActiveState_Komodo" title="ActiveState Komodo">
        ActiveState Komodo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/KDevelop" title="KDevelop">
        KDevelop
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lazarus_(software)" title="Lazarus (software)">
        Lazarus
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/MonoDevelop" title="MonoDevelop">
        MonoDevelop
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/NetBeans" title="NetBeans">
        NetBeans
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Qt_Creator" title="Qt Creator">
        Qt Creator
       </a>
       , while the long-established editors
       <a href="/wiki/Vim_(text_editor)" title="Vim (text editor)">
        Vim
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_nano" title="GNU nano">
        nano
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Emacs" title="Emacs">
        Emacs
       </a>
       remain popular.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hardware_support">
        Hardware support
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Hardware support">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg/500px-Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg/750px-Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg/1000px-Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg.png 2x" width="500"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Linux is ubiquitously found on various types of hardware.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Linux-supported_computer_architectures" title="List of Linux-supported computer architectures">
        List of Linux-supported computer architectures
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Linux kernel is a widely
       <a href="/wiki/Porting" title="Porting">
        ported
       </a>
       operating system kernel, available for devices ranging from mobile phones to supercomputers; it runs on a highly diverse range of
       <a href="/wiki/Computer_architecture" title="Computer architecture">
        computer architectures
       </a>
       , including the hand-held
       <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture" title="ARM architecture">
        ARM
       </a>
       -based
       <a href="/wiki/IPAQ" title="IPAQ">
        iPAQ
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">
        IBM
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Mainframe_computer" title="Mainframe computer">
        mainframes
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/IBM_System_z9" title="IBM System z9">
        System z9
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/IBM_System_z9" title="IBM System z9">
        System z10
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Specialized distributions and kernel forks exist for less mainstream architectures; for example, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ELKS" title="ELKS">
        ELKS
       </a>
       kernel
       <a href="/wiki/Fork_(software_development)" title="Fork (software development)">
        fork
       </a>
       can run on
       <a href="/wiki/Intel_8086" title="Intel 8086">
        Intel 8086
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Intel_80286" title="Intel 80286">
        Intel 80286
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/16-bit" title="16-bit">
        16-bit
       </a>
       microprocessors, while the
       <a href="/wiki/%CE%9CClinux" title="ΜClinux">
        µClinux
       </a>
       kernel fork may run on systems without a
       <a href="/wiki/Memory_management_unit" title="Memory management unit">
        memory management unit
       </a>
       . The kernel also runs on architectures that were only ever intended to use a manufacturer-created operating system, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">
        Macintosh
       </a>
       computers (with both
       <a href="/wiki/PowerPC" title="PowerPC">
        PowerPC
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">
        Intel
       </a>
       processors),
       <a href="/wiki/Personal_digital_assistant" title="Personal digital assistant">
        PDAs
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">
        video game consoles
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_audio_player" title="Digital audio player">
        portable music players
       </a>
       , and mobile phones.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are several industry associations and hardware
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Business_conference" title="Business conference">
        conferences
       </a>
       devoted to maintaining and improving support for diverse hardware under Linux, such as
       <a href="/wiki/FreedomHEC" title="FreedomHEC">
        FreedomHEC
       </a>
       . Over time, support for different hardware has improved in Linux, resulting in any off-the-shelf purchase having a "good chance" of being compatible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">
        Uses
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Uses">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_range_of_use" title="Linux range of use">
        Linux range of use
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Besides the Linux distributions designed for general-purpose use on desktops and servers, distributions may be specialized for different purposes including:
       <a href="/wiki/Computer_architecture" title="Computer architecture">
        computer architecture
       </a>
       support,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embedded_Linux" title="Embedded Linux">
        embedded systems
       </a>
       , stability, security, localization to a specific region or language, targeting of specific user groups, support for
       <a href="/wiki/Real-time_computing" title="Real-time computing">
        real-time
       </a>
       applications, or commitment to a given desktop environment. Furthermore, some distributions deliberately include only
       <a href="/wiki/Free_software" title="Free software">
        free software
       </a>
       . As of 2015
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , over four hundred Linux distributions are actively developed, with about a dozen distributions being most popular for general-purpose use.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Desktop">
        Desktop
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Desktop">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Free_and_open-source-software_display_servers_and_UI_toolkits.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1573" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Free_and_open-source-software_display_servers_and_UI_toolkits.svg/500px-Free_and_open-source-software_display_servers_and_UI_toolkits.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Free_and_open-source-software_display_servers_and_UI_toolkits.svg/750px-Free_and_open-source-software_display_servers_and_UI_toolkits.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Free_and_open-source-software_display_servers_and_UI_toolkits.svg/1000px-Free_and_open-source-software_display_servers_and_UI_toolkits.svg.png 2x" width="500"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Free_and_open-source-software_display_servers_and_UI_toolkits.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Visible software components of the Linux desktop stack include the
         <a href="/wiki/Display_server" title="Display server">
          display server
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Widget_engine" title="Widget engine">
          widget engines
         </a>
         , and some of the more widespread
         <a href="/wiki/Widget_toolkit" title="Widget toolkit">
          widget toolkits
         </a>
         . There are also components not directly visible to end users, including
         <a href="/wiki/D-Bus" title="D-Bus">
          D-Bus
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/PulseAudio" title="PulseAudio">
          PulseAudio
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Desktop_environment" title="Desktop environment">
        Desktop environment
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_adoption#DESKTOP" title="Linux adoption">
        Linux adoption: Measuring desktop adoption
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The popularity of Linux on standard desktop computers and laptops has been increasing over the years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most modern distributions include a graphical user environment, with, as of February 2015
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , the two most popular environments being the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KDE_Plasma_Desktop" title="KDE Plasma Desktop">
        KDE Plasma Desktop
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Xfce" title="Xfce">
        Xfce
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       No single official Linux desktop exists: rather desktop environments and Linux distributions select components from a pool of
       <a href="/wiki/Free_and_open-source_software" title="Free and open-source software">
        free and open-source software
       </a>
       with which they construct a GUI implementing some more or less strict design guide. GNOME, for example, has its
       <a href="/wiki/Human_interface_guidelines" title="Human interface guidelines">
        human interface guidelines
       </a>
       as a design guide, which gives the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human%E2%80%93machine_interface" title="Human–machine interface">
        human–machine interface
       </a>
       an important role, not just when doing the graphical design, but also when considering people with
       <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">
        disabilities
       </a>
       , and even when focusing on security.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The collaborative nature of free software development allows distributed teams to perform
       <a href="/wiki/Language_localisation" title="Language localisation">
        language localization
       </a>
       of some Linux distributions for use in locales where localizing proprietary systems would not be cost-effective. For example, the
       <a href="/wiki/Sinhalese_language" title="Sinhalese language">
        Sinhalese language
       </a>
       version of the
       <a href="/wiki/Knoppix" title="Knoppix">
        Knoppix
       </a>
       distribution became available significantly before Microsoft translated
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_XP" title="Windows XP">
        Windows XP
       </a>
       into Sinhalese.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In this case the
       <a href="/wiki/Lanka_Linux_User_Group" title="Lanka Linux User Group">
        Lanka Linux User Group
       </a>
       played a major part in developing the localized system by combining the knowledge of university professors,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguist" title="Linguist">
        linguists
       </a>
       , and local developers.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Performance_and_applications">
        Performance and applications
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Performance and applications">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The performance of Linux on the desktop has been a controversial topic;
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       for example in 2007
       <a href="/wiki/Con_Kolivas" title="Con Kolivas">
        Con Kolivas
       </a>
       accused the Linux community of favoring performance on servers. He quit Linux kernel development out of frustration with this lack of focus on the desktop, and then gave a "tell all" interview on the topic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since then a significant amount of development has focused on improving the desktop experience. Projects such as
       <a href="/wiki/Upstart" title="Upstart">
        Upstart
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Systemd" title="Systemd">
        systemd
       </a>
       aim for a faster boot time; the Wayland and Mir projects aim at replacing
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X11" title="X11">
        X11
       </a>
       while enhancing desktop performance, security and appearance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many popular applications are available for a wide variety of operating systems. For example,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mozilla_Firefox" title="Mozilla Firefox">
        Mozilla Firefox
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/OpenOffice.org" title="OpenOffice.org">
        OpenOffice.org
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/LibreOffice" title="LibreOffice">
        LibreOffice
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Blender_(software)" title="Blender (software)">
        Blender
       </a>
       have downloadable versions for all major operating systems. Furthermore, some applications initially developed for Linux, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Pidgin_(software)" title="Pidgin (software)">
        Pidgin
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/GIMP" title="GIMP">
        GIMP
       </a>
       , were ported to other operating systems (including
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">
        Windows
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mac_OS_X" title="Mac OS X">
        Mac OS X
       </a>
       ) due to their popularity. In addition, a growing number of proprietary desktop applications are also supported on Linux,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_(software)" title="Maya (software)">
        Autodesk Maya
       </a>
       , Softimage XSI and
       <a href="/wiki/Shake_(software)" title="Shake (software)">
        Apple Shake
       </a>
       in the high-end field of animation and visual effects; see the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_proprietary_software_for_Linux" title="List of proprietary software for Linux">
        list of proprietary software for Linux
       </a>
       for more details. There are also
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_gaming#Commercial_games" title="Linux gaming">
        several companies
       </a>
       that have ported their own or other companies' games to Linux, with Linux also being a supported platform on both the popular
       <a href="/wiki/Steam_(software)" title="Steam (software)">
        Steam
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Desura" title="Desura">
        Desura
       </a>
       digital-distribution services.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many other types of applications available for Microsoft Windows and Mac OS X also run on Linux. Commonly, either a
       <a href="/wiki/Free_software" title="Free software">
        free software
       </a>
       application will exist which does the functions of an application found on another operating system, or that application will have a version that works on Linux, such as with
       <a href="/wiki/Skype" title="Skype">
        Skype
       </a>
       and some
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_gaming#Commercial_games" title="Linux gaming">
        video games
       </a>
       like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dota_2" title="Dota 2">
         Dota 2
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Team_Fortress_2" title="Team Fortress 2">
         Team Fortress 2
        </a>
       </i>
       . Furthermore, the
       <a href="/wiki/Wine_(software)" title="Wine (software)">
        Wine
       </a>
       project provides a Windows compatibility layer to run unmodified Windows applications on Linux. It is sponsored by commercial interests including
       <a href="/wiki/CodeWeavers" title="CodeWeavers">
        CodeWeavers
       </a>
       , which produces a commercial version of the software. Since 2009, Google has also provided funding to the Wine project.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CrossOver" title="CrossOver">
        CrossOver
       </a>
       , a proprietary solution based on the open-source Wine project, supports running Windows versions of
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office" title="Microsoft Office">
        Microsoft Office
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Intuit" title="Intuit">
        Intuit
       </a>
       applications such as
       <a href="/wiki/Quicken" title="Quicken">
        Quicken
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/QuickBooks" title="QuickBooks">
        QuickBooks
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Photoshop" title="Adobe Photoshop">
        Adobe Photoshop
       </a>
       versions through CS2, and many popular games such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/World_of_Warcraft" title="World of Warcraft">
         World of Warcraft
        </a>
       </i>
       . In other cases, where there is no Linux port of some software in areas such as
       <a href="/wiki/Desktop_publishing" title="Desktop publishing">
        desktop publishing
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Professional_audio" title="Professional audio">
        professional audio
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       there is equivalent software available on Linux. It is also possible to run applications written for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Android_OS" title="Android OS">
        Android
       </a>
       on other versions of Linux using
       <a href="/wiki/Anbox" title="Anbox">
        Anbox
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Components_and_installation">
        Components and installation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Components and installation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Besides externally visible components, such as
       <a href="/wiki/X_window_manager" title="X window manager">
        X window managers
       </a>
       , a non-obvious but quite central role is played by the programs hosted by
       <a href="/wiki/Freedesktop.org" title="Freedesktop.org">
        freedesktop.org
       </a>
       , such as
       <a href="/wiki/D-Bus" title="D-Bus">
        D-Bus
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/PulseAudio" title="PulseAudio">
        PulseAudio
       </a>
       ; both major desktop environments (GNOME and KDE) include them, each offering graphical front-ends written using the corresponding toolkit (
       <a href="/wiki/GTK%2B" title="GTK+">
        GTK+
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qt_(framework)" title="Qt (framework)">
        Qt
       </a>
       ). A
       <a href="/wiki/Display_server" title="Display server">
        display server
       </a>
       is another component, which for the longest time has been communicating in the X11 display server protocol with its clients; prominent software talking X11 includes the
       <a href="/wiki/X.Org_Server" title="X.Org Server">
        X.Org Server
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Xlib" title="Xlib">
        Xlib
       </a>
       . Frustration over the cumbersome X11 core protocol, and especially over its numerous extensions, has led to the creation of a new display server protocol,
       <a href="/wiki/Wayland_(display_server_protocol)" title="Wayland (display server protocol)">
        Wayland
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Installing, updating and removing software in Linux is typically done through the use of package managers such as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synaptic_Package_Manager" title="Synaptic Package Manager">
        Synaptic Package Manager
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/PackageKit" title="PackageKit">
        PackageKit
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yellow_dog_Updater,_Modified" title="Yellow dog Updater, Modified">
        Yum Extender
       </a>
       . While most major Linux distributions have extensive repositories, often containing tens of thousands of packages, not all the software that can run on Linux is available from the official repositories. Alternatively, users can install packages from unofficial repositories, download pre-compiled packages directly from websites, or compile the source code by themselves. All these methods come with different degrees of difficulty; compiling the source code is in general considered a challenging process for new Linux users, but it is hardly needed in modern distributions and is not a method specific to Linux.
      </p>
